<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_d0b651a4-8a7b-4488-9e77-4b80f0e98440'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer5987204921674997689"><p>Probably the most popular book in the history of the Far East, this classic sixteenth century novel is a combination of picaresque novel and folk epic that mixes satire, allegory, and history into a rollicking adventure. It is the story of the roguish Monkey and his encounters with major and minor spirits, gods, demigods, demons, ogres, monsters, and fairies. This translat</p></span>
  <span id="freeText5987204921674997689" style="display:none"><p>Probably the most popular book in the history of the Far East, this classic sixteenth century novel is a combination of picaresque novel and folk epic that mixes satire, allegory, and history into a rollicking adventure. It is the story of the roguish Monkey and his encounters with major and minor spirits, gods, demigods, demons, ogres, monsters, and fairies. This translation, by the distinguished scholar Arthur Waley, is the first accurate English version; it makes available to the Western reader a faithful reproduction of the spirit and meaning of the original.</p></span>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">There are two complete translations of "Journey to the West". One is by William John Francis Jenner and is published by China's Foreign Language Press<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">There are two complete translations of "Journey to the West". One is by William John Francis Jenner and is published by China's Foreign Language Press. The other, recently revised, is by Anthony C. Yu and is published by the University of Chicago Press.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_72656361" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/72656361" />
    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/827856-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1255806840p2/827856.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/72656361?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve" href="/user/show/827856-steve">Steve</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer72656361" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18217003772530701620">I kind of regret buying this book. I thought it looked like a fun little read when I saw it in the mythology section, so I picked it up (several years ago).<br/><br/>Why regret it when I enjoyed it? I could have enjoyed MORE of it. You see, I found out much later that Monkey is an abridged version of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/158788.The_Journey_to_the_West__Volume_1__Journey_to_the_West_' title='The Journey to the West, Volume 1 (Journey to the West) by Wu Cheng'en' rel='nofollow noopener'>Journey to the West</a>. This is one of the four classic Chinese novels. I've read (and generally loved) the other three: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/158771.Romance_of_the_Three_Kingdoms__Vol__1' title='Romance of the Three Kingdoms, Vol. 1 by Luo Guanzhong' rel='nofollow noopener'>Romance of the Three Kingdoms</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/158778.Outlaws_of_the_Marsh__Vol__1_4_' title='Outlaws of the Marsh (Vol. 1-4) by Shi Nai'an' rel='nofollow noopener'>Outlaws of the Marsh</a>, and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/535739.The_Dream_of_the_Red_Chamber' title='The Dream of the Red Chamber by Cao Xueqin' rel='nofollow noopener'>Dream of Red Mansions</a>. Now I've r</span>
  <span id="freeText18217003772530701620" style="display:none">I kind of regret buying this book. I thought it looked like a fun little read when I saw it in the mythology section, so I picked it up (several years ago).<br /><br />Why regret it when I enjoyed it? I could have enjoyed MORE of it. You see, I found out much later that Monkey is an abridged version of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/158788.The_Journey_to_the_West__Volume_1__Journey_to_the_West_" title="The Journey to the West, Volume 1 (Journey to the West) by Wu Cheng'en" rel="nofollow noopener">Journey to the West</a>. This is one of the four classic Chinese novels. I've read (and generally loved) the other three: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/158771.Romance_of_the_Three_Kingdoms__Vol__1" title="Romance of the Three Kingdoms, Vol. 1 by Luo Guanzhong" rel="nofollow noopener">Romance of the Three Kingdoms</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/158778.Outlaws_of_the_Marsh__Vol__1_4_" title="Outlaws of the Marsh (Vol. 1-4) by Shi Nai'an" rel="nofollow noopener">Outlaws of the Marsh</a>, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/535739.The_Dream_of_the_Red_Chamber" title="The Dream of the Red Chamber by Cao Xueqin" rel="nofollow noopener">Dream of Red Mansions</a>. Now I've read an abridged version of the last one, when I would have much preferred to read the full unabridged text. I'll still have to do that at some point.<br /><br />Still, I can recommend this book pretty enthusiastically to some people at there. Reading the other three books mentioned above, I undoubtedly tried to sell you on them (directly or indirectly). Perhaps you were even a bit interested. <br /><br />However, I recognize that the other three, thousand+ page monsters can be pretty intimidating, particularly since they feature so many characters with names that are difficult to pronounce and keep straight if you are not particularly familiar with Chinese names. Monkey is only about three hundred pages, and style wise is a much easier read as well. There are fewer important characters, and they have more easily pronounced/remembered names (Monkey and Pigsy being two of the main four characters). This story is also quite a bit more of a folk tale than the others, so it remains noticeably simpler. That said, it retains the very classic style that I haven't seen anywhere besides these Chinese novels. The charmingly formal well that people address one another (even the taunts before battles are formalized in a very unique way). This would be a great book to use as your trial run into classic Chinese literature, and if you enjoy the general style of it, you will enjoy the style of the longer and more difficult books as well.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18217003772530701620" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_1262286235" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1262286235" />
    <a title="Roy Lotz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz"><img alt="Roy Lotz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443324405p2/14046996.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1262286235?book_show_action=true">Sep 07, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roy Lotz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roy Lotz" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz">Roy Lotz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=novels-novellas-short-stories">novels-novellas-short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=orientalism">orientalism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1262286235" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6770709403526420325">Perhaps because I have been reading too many political books—inevitably dreary—I found <i>Monkey</i> to be almost electrifyingly delightful, from the beginning to the very end. When I bought the book used, on a whim, I knew virtually nothing about it other than it was a famous Chinese classic. Thus, I vaguely expected something rather dry and edifying; so I was delighted was, instead, I found the inspiration for one of my great childhood loves, <i>Dragon Ball</i>. Indeed, while admittedly lacking in animation</span>
  <span id="freeText6770709403526420325" style="display:none">Perhaps because I have been reading too many political books—inevitably dreary—I found <i>Monkey</i> to be almost electrifyingly delightful, from the beginning to the very end. When I bought the book used, on a whim, I knew virtually nothing about it other than it was a famous Chinese classic. Thus, I vaguely expected something rather dry and edifying; so I was delighted was, instead, I found the inspiration for one of my great childhood loves, <i>Dragon Ball</i>. Indeed, while admittedly lacking in animation, Monkey is just as silly and wonderful as Goku’s exploits—with the added benefit of giving you bragging rights for reading it.<br /><br />Well, perhaps I should not feel too proud, as Monkey is an abridgement of a much larger work, Journey to the West, which is more than six times longer. But as the original book is episodic—consisting of misadventure after misadventure on the way to India—the reading experience of this shorter version is seamless, as it merely consists of fewer episodes.<br /><br />Authorship of this book is normally attributed Wu Cheng’en, who seems a rather tepid character in comparison to his book. But Wu—or whoever the author was—had ample material to work with. By the time that Journey to the West was written, there was already a very old oral tradition concerning the 7th century Buddhist pilgrim, Xuanzang (called Tripitaka in this version), and his many trials on the way to India. The author’s accomplishment consists in arranging these many stories into a coherent whole, and telling them in lively, colloquial prose.<br />	<br />The closest European counterpart I can think of is <i>Gargantua and Pantagruel</i>, by Rabelais, which was written at very close to the same time. Both books are absurd and strikingly irreverent, and filled with gusto and a zest for life. But of course, the parallel is not exact. While both works parody conventional politics and religion, Rabelais’s work is more thoroughly earthy, while Wu’s does have higher, spiritual resonances, especially as the book progresses. Indeed, I admit I felt slightly holy myself by the time I put it down, as I read it during my own little pilgrimage on the Camino de Santiago. The book was a perfect companion</span>
  <a data-text-id="6770709403526420325" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3076733233" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3076733233" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1385615-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536121261p2/1385615.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3076733233?book_show_action=true">Dec 16, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/1385615-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3076733233" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7139905305618246344">Kudos to Arthur Waley for somehow reducing this 100-chapter classic of ancient Chinese literature into a 30-chapter abridgment that makes sense. Certainly many hard choices were made along the way. There is almost none of the florid court poetry that the original has in abundance, and many fun adventures wound up on the cutting room floor, but what remains captures the spirit, humor, suspense, and moral lessons of Wu Cheng'en's "Xi You Ji" (Journey to the West).<br/><br/>Because this was published in 1943</span>
  <span id="freeText7139905305618246344" style="display:none">Kudos to Arthur Waley for somehow reducing this 100-chapter classic of ancient Chinese literature into a 30-chapter abridgment that makes sense. Certainly many hard choices were made along the way. There is almost none of the florid court poetry that the original has in abundance, and many fun adventures wound up on the cutting room floor, but what remains captures the spirit, humor, suspense, and moral lessons of Wu Cheng'en's "Xi You Ji" (Journey to the West).<br /><br />Because this was published in 1943, all spellings follow the Wade-Giles guidelines instead of the cleaner, truer Hanyu Pinyin. This will hardly matter to readers with little knowledge of Chinese pronunciation, but I'm not a big fan of the earlier British Imperial language system for Mandarin and it slowed me down.<br /><br />Waley also chooses quaint names for his quartet of seekers and loses any subtlety or richness in the process. Zhu Bajie (Eight Precepts Swine) becomes "Pigsy", for example. The priest Tang Sanzang becomes "Tripitaka" rather than "Hsuan Tsang". Tripitaka is "Three Baskets" (the very Buddhist Scripture this priest and his disciples are traveling to India to receive) and, as applied to this questing monk, is dry and reductive. It's rather like translating "Moses" as "The Tablets". I wasn't a big fan of this choice, either.<br /><br />All in all, a 3.5-star treatment of a 5-star story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7139905305618246344" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer45323438" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13430743939569951579">"Monkey" is Arthur Waley's delightful rendition of Wu Cheng-en's "Journey to the West", one of China's four great classical novels. This abridged version provides English readers with an experience that would otherwise have been inaccessible to those of us unable to read the original Chinese. The novel offers a pleasant mixture of action, adventure and comedy. It examines a number of meaningful themes, including three great Eastern philosophies (Buddhism, Tao and Confucianism) and satirical comm</span>
  <span id="freeText13430743939569951579" style="display:none">"Monkey" is Arthur Waley's delightful rendition of Wu Cheng-en's "Journey to the West", one of China's four great classical novels. This abridged version provides English readers with an experience that would otherwise have been inaccessible to those of us unable to read the original Chinese. The novel offers a pleasant mixture of action, adventure and comedy. It examines a number of meaningful themes, including three great Eastern philosophies (Buddhism, Tao and Confucianism) and satirical commentary on their failed practice by people in an overly bureaucratic society who miss the forest for the trees. A must read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13430743939569951579" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1067916253" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1067916253" />
    <a title="Graham" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1760824-graham"><img alt="Graham" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1380743323p2/1760824.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1067916253?book_show_action=true">Sep 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Graham" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Graham" href="/user/show/1760824-graham">Graham</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140441116" href="/book/show/190065.Monkey">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1760824-graham?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1760824-graham?shelf=asia">asia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1760824-graham?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1067916253" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1162146508114202453">The last thing I'd expect a hundreds-of-years-old slice of classic Chinese literature to be is fun, but that's exactly what MONKEY is. It's great fun! It's a delight to read, a thoroughly modern action-adventure storyline that embodies the classic 'journey' narrative and packs it to the brim with all manner of outlandish incident and constant humour.<br/><br/>The only difficulty with MONKEY comes from trying to remember all of the various deities and sub-sections that Heaven is made up of. Almost every ch</span>
  <span id="freeText1162146508114202453" style="display:none">The last thing I'd expect a hundreds-of-years-old slice of classic Chinese literature to be is fun, but that's exactly what MONKEY is. It's great fun! It's a delight to read, a thoroughly modern action-adventure storyline that embodies the classic 'journey' narrative and packs it to the brim with all manner of outlandish incident and constant humour.<br /><br />The only difficulty with MONKEY comes from trying to remember all of the various deities and sub-sections that Heaven is made up of. Almost every character in the story is divine in some way, and that's overwhelming at first, but the more you read, the more it all makes sense. Monkey himself is a great protagonist; he starts off as completely annoying, but the reader gradually warms to him as the narrative progresses. Monkey never changes, but the reader gets to know and like him instead. The rest of the characters, Tripitaka, Pigsy, and Sandy, are built to entertain.<br /><br />Arthur Waley's translation is a joy to read, and probably the most readable version of a 16th century story that you'll ever find. It's also surprisingly modern in places, complete with back-stabbing, betrayal and low brow humour. The story is tumultuous and fantastic and yes, epic in the true sense of the word; the only problem is that this is an abridged version of a much, much longer original, and thus it makes you long to read the full-length version.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1162146508114202453" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_841932856" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/841932856" />
    <a title="Jim Peterson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24498353-jim-peterson"><img alt="Jim Peterson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1381082195p2/24498353.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/841932856?book_show_action=true">Feb 02, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim Peterson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim Peterson" href="/user/show/24498353-jim-peterson">Jim Peterson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24498353-jim-peterson?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24498353-jim-peterson?shelf=wuxia">wuxia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24498353-jim-peterson?shelf=historic-fiction">historic-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer841932856" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11742855488123174915">Monkey is a magical tale of fantasy and adventure in the Tang Dynasty (618–907) of imperial China. At around 350 pages, this translation is actually a short version of the 2,000-some-page Journey to the West, which was written in the 16th century. It is a very important book throughout Asia, and considered one of the four Great Classical Novels of Chinese literature. A Japanese friend of mine assures me that 98% of Asians know the story of Journey to the West whether through the book directly or</span>
  <span id="freeText11742855488123174915" style="display:none">Monkey is a magical tale of fantasy and adventure in the Tang Dynasty (618–907) of imperial China. At around 350 pages, this translation is actually a short version of the 2,000-some-page Journey to the West, which was written in the 16th century. It is a very important book throughout Asia, and considered one of the four Great Classical Novels of Chinese literature. A Japanese friend of mine assures me that 98% of Asians know the story of Journey to the West whether through the book directly or its numerous spin-offs.<br /><br />Although Monkey is an abridgement, it doesn’t read like one. It really feels like a full story. Most of what was omitted consists of individual adventures along the pilgrims’ journey to India to fetch Buddhist scriptures. Since these mini adventures are largely self-contained, you don’t notice their absence when reading, although the ending does come off as somewhat abrupt.<br /><br />I’ve been wanting to read some Wuxia for a long time due to my personal interest in martial arts. Wuxia is basically Chinese martial fiction, and it is hard to find anything in this genre with less than 2,000 pages. I specifically chose this abridged version because I wanted to get a soft start rather than dive right into a 2,000-page brick only to give up. <br /><br />Though the translation is not perfect, the style is sometimes archaic and the ebook version contains some digital transfer errors, Monkey still fulfilled my expectations. And I expect this won’t be my last wuxia novel. Despite the drawbacks, I'm giving this five starts because I know I'm going to be thinking about this story for a long time.<br /><br />Note:<br />While it definitely helps to first have some basic knowledge of Buddhist philosophy and terms (i.e., the difference between Buddhas, Bodhisattvas and Arhats) and the major figures (Guatama/Sakyamuni, Kwan Yin, Amitabha and the Taoist Lao Tzu), you could easily get by without any such prior knowledge and probably learn a good deal about Chinese beliefs simply by reading this book. <br /><br />Interesting trivia: Dragon Ball is based on Journey to the West. The Monkey King is called Sun Wukong in Chinese and Son Goku in Japanese. Hence the name of Goku in Dragon Ball, who is based on the Monkey King. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11742855488123174915" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1219303278" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1219303278" />
    <a title="Lara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10202231-lara"><img alt="Lara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586147712p2/10202231.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1219303278?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lara" href="/user/show/10202231-lara">Lara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10202231-lara?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10202231-lara?shelf=buddhism">buddhism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10202231-lara?shelf=taoism">taoism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10202231-lara?shelf=religious-mythology">religious-mythology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1219303278" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5248573749831153058">I'm embarrassed to admit that I learned only recently about Sun Wukong, a very famous monkey character all over Asia. That is to say, billions of people on earth are quite familiar with Sun Wukong, and I didn't know he existed until about a year ago! The planet is becoming smaller and smaller, but there are still some East/West divides... <br/><br/>In any case, the "monkey" of the title is Sun Wukong. This story, which is so well known is Asia, is usually known as "The Journey to the West" (without "monke</span>
  <span id="freeText5248573749831153058" style="display:none">I'm embarrassed to admit that I learned only recently about Sun Wukong, a very famous monkey character all over Asia. That is to say, billions of people on earth are quite familiar with Sun Wukong, and I didn't know he existed until about a year ago! The planet is becoming smaller and smaller, but there are still some East/West divides... <br /><br />In any case, the "monkey" of the title is Sun Wukong. This story, which is so well known is Asia, is usually known as "The Journey to the West" (without "monkey" in the title). The story, credited to Wu Cheng'en, dates to the 16th century. This version was translated by Arthur Waley, a British scholar, in the mid 20th century. <br /><br />I'm so glad to have read this tale. It's hard to describe; my one sentence summary would be "The Ramayana meets Don Quixote". What does that mean? It reminds me of Don Quixote in that it was written hundreds of years ago in a land far away, yet parts of it are laugh-out-loud funny to this 21st century American. It reminds me of the Ramayana in that it has an epic scope (characters include the Buddha, Kwon Yin, Lao Tzu, etc.), and a powerful monkey is in the mix trying to do the right thing to serve his master. (In the Ramayana, it is "monkey"/vanara Hanuman who serves Ram; here, Sun Wukong is primarily serving the Buddha. Unlike Hanuman, who is quite earnest, Sun Wukong is a scamp/trickster and much more morally ambiguous than dear Hanuman.) The "journey to the west" in question is a trip from China to India to fetch some scriptures.<br /><br />Why four stars instead of five? There are times when the writing feels stilted to me. I'm guessing that Waley was trying to translate literally (as literally as one can translate from Chinese to English, that is), and so the prose at times feels dense.<br /><br />I'm very glad to have read this; I'm very glad that I have now joined the billions of people who know this whimsical tale.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5248573749831153058" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3121399506" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3121399506" />
    <a title="John_Dishwasher" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44246464-john-dishwasher"><img alt="John_Dishwasher" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543641832p2/44246464.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3121399506?book_show_action=true">Jan 05, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John_Dishwasher" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John_Dishwasher" href="/user/show/44246464-john-dishwasher">John_Dishwasher</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3121399506" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10988160981202571525">The way this tale plays out reminds me of legends surrounding holy relics, or supposed events that confer significance upon hallowed grounds. So possibly this is an origin story for some sacred scriptures somewhere. Mostly, though, the book is a balls-out supernatural adventure. At times it reminded me of all the superhero movies we’re being fed right now by mainstream media; and this parallel led me to realize that in today’s movies we are being shown convincing representations of supernatural </span>
  <span id="freeText10988160981202571525" style="display:none">The way this tale plays out reminds me of legends surrounding holy relics, or supposed events that confer significance upon hallowed grounds. So possibly this is an origin story for some sacred scriptures somewhere. Mostly, though, the book is a balls-out supernatural adventure. At times it reminded me of all the superhero movies we’re being fed right now by mainstream media; and this parallel led me to realize that in today’s movies we are being shown convincing representations of supernatural feats that have fascinated the human imagination for millenia. Now wonder they so intoxicate us! <br /><br />Part of what holds the chaos of this story together, though, and keeps it pleasurable, is the sense that there is a kind of architecture beneath it, some guiding principle. Without this feeling I think it would lose its cohesion and momentum. For <i>Monkey</i> is a so-called ‘folk novel’ (something I’ve never heard of), which includes battling gods and monsters and kings and savage animals and even fighting planets. Probably it is meant to be instructive, to intentionally teach precepts of Buddhism. I’m thinking that’s where the feeling of structure comes from. The Monkey goes through a definite transformation as he starts as a wild creature running amok, undergoes a period of restraint and penance, and then becomes a champion of wisdom. Perhaps his journey is the journey we all go through as we tame our instincts toward maturity, or holiness, or even a comfortable pension. <br /><br />My favorite scenes are the ‘contests of transformation,’ which happen twice, where competing adversaries shapeshift into different forms as they fight, constantly escalating their powers through the creatures they assume. I’d love to see that stuff on film. <br /><br />A quote: “Lift Mount T’ai, it’s as light as a mustard seed, but don’t try to raise a mortal above the Earthly dust.”<br /><br />I read this for free on the Internet Archive.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10988160981202571525" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9306990947336466585">i'm very sorry that i didn't like this more. many people seem to think this is a good translation, which disappoints me because i was quite willing to lay the blame at my inability to get into this book on arthur waley though it may be that they are lauding the book for its accuracy in translation rather than in its artistry. i'm not sure why i didn't enjoy it as much as i didn't: i love folklore, and monsters and fighting and adventures but despite all that, this book's take on those things kep</span>
  <span id="freeText9306990947336466585" style="display:none">i'm very sorry that i didn't like this more. many people seem to think this is a good translation, which disappoints me because i was quite willing to lay the blame at my inability to get into this book on arthur waley though it may be that they are lauding the book for its accuracy in translation rather than in its artistry. i'm not sure why i didn't enjoy it as much as i didn't: i love folklore, and monsters and fighting and adventures but despite all that, this book's take on those things kept making me want to pass out every time i read it. it's abridged but it still felt really long, and inconsistent, and repetitive. the only thing that really interested me was when the priest tripitaka lied to monkey with ease even though he was very pious about not eating meat, or doing other things that were contrary to what he had learned in buddhist monastery. <br /><br />it may be that i just don't get it, i don't know. maybe i am just too ensconced in the traditions of western literature to really appreciate it. but it just fell flat. </span>

<div id="review_228259377" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/228259377" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/228259377?book_show_action=true">Oct 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

          marked it as to-read




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=china">china</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer228259377" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16402987675270218300">Waley's abridged version is widely...tolerated at least, liked by very many. <br/><br/>There is also <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/158792.The_Monkey_and_the_Monk?from_search=true' rel='nofollow noopener'>this abridged version</a> of the Yu translation: slightly longer at 528 pages. <br/><br/>Copying directly from Wendy - sorry, Wendy, it's just that it was really interesting:<blockquote>The most popular, though much-abridged version (in translation anyway?) is <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/100237.Monkey__The_Journey_to_the_West' title='Monkey: The Journey to the West by Wu Cheng'en' rel='nofollow noopener'>Monkey: The Journey to the West</a>. I did some research and have decided on this non-abridged version instead: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13593194.The_Journey_to_the_West__Volume_1' title='The Journey to the West, Volume 1 by Wu Cheng'en' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Journey to the West, Volume 1</a> and just take it on one volume a</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText16402987675270218300" style="display:none">Waley's abridged version is widely...tolerated at least, liked by very many. <br /><br />There is also <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/158792.The_Monkey_and_the_Monk?from_search=true" rel="nofollow noopener">this abridged version</a> of the Yu translation: slightly longer at 528 pages. <br /><br />Copying directly from Wendy - sorry, Wendy, it's just that it was really interesting:<blockquote>The most popular, though much-abridged version (in translation anyway?) is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/100237.Monkey__The_Journey_to_the_West" title="Monkey: The Journey to the West by Wu Cheng'en" rel="nofollow noopener">Monkey: The Journey to the West</a>. I did some research and have decided on this non-abridged version instead: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13593194.The_Journey_to_the_West__Volume_1" title="The Journey to the West, Volume 1 by Wu Cheng'en" rel="nofollow noopener">The Journey to the West, Volume 1</a> and just take it on one volume at a time.<br /><br />There's a great video with Anthony Yu, the translator of the above Journey to the West, Vol. 1, (<a href="http://asiasociety.org/video/education/anthony-yu-journey-west" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">http://asiasociety.org/video/educatio...</a>) addressing the Asia Society. During the bombings his grandfather had distracted him with Journey to the West during the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong. He tells a great anecdote from JttW where Monkey pees on Buddah's hand--it was the translator's favorite part as a boy (of course!). Anyway, the video is 50 minutes long but interesting (esp the first 20 min) &amp; definitely made me want to read it!<br /><br />Also, NYT has an archived review of Yu's translation from 1983: <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/1983/03/06/books/the-complete-monkey.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">http://www.nytimes.com/1983/03/06/boo...</a> : "The standard modern version, translated by Mr. Yu, is substantially the same as what is thought to be the first edition, in 100 chapters, published (the author was anonymous) at Nanjing in 1592. (Mr. Yu's version differs from this mainly by the addition of a single episode, drawn from a short version of the novel dating to about the same era.)</blockquote>That's alllll from Wendy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16402987675270218300" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/312216857" />
    <a title="Missy J" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7406091-missy-j"><img alt="Missy J" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1481102743p2/7406091.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

<span id="freeTextContainer4458854547072874074"><b>July 12th, 2016 Review:</b> FOUR STARS<br/><br/>I think I read a different book four years ago. It definitely was a different translation.<br/><br/>This time around, I really enjoyed the Journey to the West, or better known as "Monkey" translated by Arthur Waley.<br/>The story was easy to follow and quite funny.   <span id="freeText4458854547072874074" style="display:none"><b>July 12th, 2016 Review:</b> FOUR STARS<br /><br />I think I read a different book four years ago. It definitely was a different translation.<br /><br />This time around, I really enjoyed the Journey to the West, or better known as "Monkey" translated by Arthur Waley.<br />The story was easy to follow and quite funny. I never lost track of what was going on. <br />I'm glad that I give this another chance. Monkey is a powerful, ingenious rascal, whose only faults are his self-absorbed regard of himself. I especially loved the beginning of the book, when Monkey rules over his own kingdom and causes chaos in Heaven (which is supposed to symbolize the government). <br /><br />Out of the four Chinese classics, this is by far my favorite book. And it's not a long read!<br /><br /><b>"What's the use of living so long in the world if you haven't learnt even to recognise a joke when you hear one?" - Monkey</b><br /><br /><b>April 22nd, 2012 Review:</b> TWO STARS<br /><br />Guess this isn't my cup of tea. I chose this book, because I'm right now going through a "read books set in Asia" phase. Among the many books set in China, this wasn't about the communists, Cultural Revolution, torture,... It's one of China' most popular folktale. So I thought, ok I'll give it a try.<br /><br />The way the story was told was unusual, no suspense, filled with old words. It maybe the fault of the translation. Reading this reminds me of "The Fugitive", which was really not exciting as well.<br /><br />But I'm not angry that I read this book. There are good books and bad books, that's life.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer661616779" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer2171644694672467977">Fans of the fantastic 'Monkey Magic' series might enjoy reading this early English translation of the classic Chinese folk tale -- one based on historical fact.<br/><br/>This particular translation is prefaced by a very interesting essay about the translator, a Christian missionary who found (and thus inserted) various Christian messianic themes into the story.<br/><br/>Sadly, this translation has practically no characterisation; it is told as a series of events with very little drama or descriptive language.<br/><br/>Very i</span>
  <span id="freeText2171644694672467977" style="display:none">Fans of the fantastic 'Monkey Magic' series might enjoy reading this early English translation of the classic Chinese folk tale -- one based on historical fact.<br /><br />This particular translation is prefaced by a very interesting essay about the translator, a Christian missionary who found (and thus inserted) various Christian messianic themes into the story.<br /><br />Sadly, this translation has practically no characterisation; it is told as a series of events with very little drama or descriptive language.<br /><br />Very interesting if you're hoping to get closer to the source material, disappointing if you want the fun and drama of good storytelling.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13787630073391310753">This is an interesting little read that provides a small window into what is presumably a much more complete work. This is an abridgement of journey to the West and as such gives you the key plot beats but it's an allegorical novel at it's core so cutting out massive chunks of the allegory really damages the overall effect. <br/><br/>The writing style and characters are all great and it's surprisingly funny for an ancient work about Buddhism but ultimately it's quite repetitive and this provides little mo</span>
  <span id="freeText13787630073391310753" style="display:none">This is an interesting little read that provides a small window into what is presumably a much more complete work. This is an abridgement of journey to the West and as such gives you the key plot beats but it's an allegorical novel at it's core so cutting out massive chunks of the allegory really damages the overall effect. <br /><br />The writing style and characters are all great and it's surprisingly funny for an ancient work about Buddhism but ultimately it's quite repetitive and this provides little more than you would get from a brief story summary.<br /><br />It's worth a go if you're wanting to dip your toe into the Chinese classics but leaves a lot to be desired.</span>

<div id="review_3517698568" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3517698568" />
    <a title="Eli Unger" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/80710419-eli-unger"><img alt="Eli Unger" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1523916921p2/80710419.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3517698568?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eli Unger" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eli Unger" href="/user/show/80710419-eli-unger">Eli Unger</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3517698568" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15113162750723547920">The amount of charm this book has for something over 400 years old is incredible. Considering this is an English translation of Classical Chinese, it is extremely readable, the only areas of difficulty being the references to Taoist and Buddhist religious figures which were, of course, lost on this 21st century Irish feen. Despite this I found it didn't impede my overall enjoyment of the novel, which continued to entertain me throughout with that particular brand of moralistic humour and wit tha</span>
  <span id="freeText15113162750723547920" style="display:none">The amount of charm this book has for something over 400 years old is incredible. Considering this is an English translation of Classical Chinese, it is extremely readable, the only areas of difficulty being the references to Taoist and Buddhist religious figures which were, of course, lost on this 21st century Irish feen. Despite this I found it didn't impede my overall enjoyment of the novel, which continued to entertain me throughout with that particular brand of moralistic humour and wit that's so characteristic of a great folk tale. Given the huge place the Journey to the West occupies in Chinese lit and pop culture, I'd consider this a very accessible introduction to the novel, and though I have yet to brave a less abridged translation of the text, I feel like this was a great start.<br />Rating: 🐒/🐒</span>
  <a data-text-id="15113162750723547920" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_789586361" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/789586361" />
    <a title="Donovan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7565775-donovan"><img alt="Donovan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1333056008p2/7565775.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/789586361?book_show_action=true">Dec 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Donovan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Donovan" href="/user/show/7565775-donovan">Donovan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11487744-monkey">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7565775-donovan?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7565775-donovan?shelf=comedy">comedy</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer789586361" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16894715121917383275">Monkey - Great Sage and Equal of Heaven. I watched the classic Japanese TV series when I was a kid to buying the complete series on DVD so my children wouldn't miss out on the fun (And I can say it is still as good today as it was when I was young). But until now I had not read the book itself. The original was written in the 1500's by a Chinese author Wu Ch'eng-en and was called 'Journey to the West'. The original was 100 chapters long and after trying to read more scholarly translations I foun</span>
  <span id="freeText16894715121917383275" style="display:none">Monkey - Great Sage and Equal of Heaven. I watched the classic Japanese TV series when I was a kid to buying the complete series on DVD so my children wouldn't miss out on the fun (And I can say it is still as good today as it was when I was young). But until now I had not read the book itself. The original was written in the 1500's by a Chinese author Wu Ch'eng-en and was called 'Journey to the West'. The original was 100 chapters long and after trying to read more scholarly translations I found to be too literal and way too dry (It reminded me of reading the scholarly translations of Gilgamesh)I chose to go with an abridged version - and I am glad I did.<br />The version I went for is the Arthur Waley translation. It is 30 chapters long and captures the beginning and ending quite when and provides a good insight in to the characters. It was the ending for me that I really wanted to read as the TV series was cut short and I never found out what happened. The Waley version of the story keeps this story to a well paced read and includes a couple of the adventures of Tripitaka, Monkey, Pigsy, Sandy and horse that clearly define the characters, their relationships and the spirit of adventure that has made this one of the most read and adapted stories in (and out of) Asia. <br />If you have watched the TV show or want to know what the fuss is about regarding this amazing story then I recommend the Arthur Waley abridged version.<br /><br />PLOT ***Spoilers***<br />At the beginning of the novel we learn of a monkey born from a stone nourished by the Five Elements, who learns the art of the Tao, 72 polymorphic transformations, combat, and secrets of immortality, and through guile and force makes a name for himself in Heaven - 'Great Sage equal of Heaven' and to put it lightly, gets in to so much trouble that he is ultimately trapped and imprisoned by the Buddha. <br />500 years later Buddha seeks a pilgrim who will travel West, to India. The hope is to retrieve sacred scriptures by which the Chinese people may be enlightened so that their behaviour (seen as greed, hedonism, promiscuity, and sins) may accord with the tenets of Buddhism. The young monk Tripitaka (Who has his own story)volunteers to undertake the pilgrimage. <br />Along the way, Tripitaka encounters and frees Monkey, and he and Monkey thereafter recruit two more companions, Pigsy and Sandy. There is another member of the group that is encountered and takes on the name and form of a Horse (Formerly a Dragon that had been punished and cast out of his father's palace). <br />They liberate a captive princess and punish her abductor, who has also murdered her father. The father is resurrected and reinstalled as king. They meet several bodhisattvas and fight fierce monsters, make new friends, release slaves, reveal vile plots before finally arriving at Buddha's palace.<br />The scriptures are retrieved and return to China with them. They overcome some final hurdles so the the numerology of their journey is precise and finally they deliver the scriptures to the Emperor and the people of China.<br />They are then returned to be rewarded by the Buddha. Tripitaka and Monkey are made in to Buddha's. Sandy is made in to a Arhat. Horse is returned back in to Dragon form as a great Naga. And poor old Pigsy who hase always been tempered by his greed, is promoted to an altar cleanser (i.e. eater of excess offerings at altars).</span>
  <a data-text-id="16894715121917383275" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_6892305" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6892305" />
    <a title="Vanessa Fabiano" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58810-vanessa-fabiano"><img alt="Vanessa Fabiano" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560237901p2/58810.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6892305?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vanessa Fabiano" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vanessa Fabiano" href="/user/show/58810-vanessa-fabiano">Vanessa Fabiano</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6892305" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1656190590076917339">Looking for a riveting piece of 16th century Chinese folk fiction? Try the hilarious adventure tale “Monkey" (also known as Journey to the West). Penned by scholar Wu Chen An, it tells the story of a mischievous monkey, and is based on the actual pilgrimage of the monk Tripitaka to India, to fetch the Buddhist scriptures for the Tang emperor.<br/><br/>Wu layers this earnest, grueling undertaking, with legend, gossip, superstition, religion, and concocts a rollicking bit of satire. The central irony of the</span>
  <span id="freeText1656190590076917339" style="display:none">Looking for a riveting piece of 16th century Chinese folk fiction? Try the hilarious adventure tale “Monkey" (also known as Journey to the West). Penned by scholar Wu Chen An, it tells the story of a mischievous monkey, and is based on the actual pilgrimage of the monk Tripitaka to India, to fetch the Buddhist scriptures for the Tang emperor.<br /><br />Wu layers this earnest, grueling undertaking, with legend, gossip, superstition, religion, and concocts a rollicking bit of satire. The central irony of the book is that, though it is plotted around a religious pilgrimage, nothing in it is sacred: Taoism is run down as second-rate, the divine denizens of heaven are crushed by complex, impenetrable bureaucracy, Tripitaka, the monk designated for this important pilgrimage, is a sobbing, at times abject creature, and monkey, the disciple assigned to him by a Boddhisvatta, is a self-important brat, addicted to physical violence, moody, and prone to fits of savage rage. And yet, he is the undisputed star of the show, a magical genius who attains the much coveted immortality status (as Buddha Victorious in Strife).<br /><br />The other star of the book is its supreme, unflagging pragmatism. Disciples agonize about tattered frocks, philosophers fret about the cost of coal, immortals haggle about the number of transformations they are allotted, and scriptures command a hefty price tag. It is the unrelenting contrast between the surreal events of the novel - this surplus of magic - and the resolutely practical tone in which the story is told that generates its wit and narrative tension, and makes monkey such an entertaining treat.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1656190590076917339" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah " href="/user/show/3189997-sarah">Sarah </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer929480967" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8302323764327068242">I had no idea this was a an actual book, never mind translated and available in Penguin classic format! Having loved the camp TV series made in China and shown on UK TV in the late 1970s when i was a child I could not resist reading it. I am very glad I did.<br/>As we Buddhists will tell you, it is very difficult to describe the indescribable but I will try. First of all life is humourous, the best part of life is laughter, and this book has plenty of that, and what is more uses it as a gentle didact</span>
  <span id="freeText8302323764327068242" style="display:none">I had no idea this was a an actual book, never mind translated and available in Penguin classic format! Having loved the camp TV series made in China and shown on UK TV in the late 1970s when i was a child I could not resist reading it. I am very glad I did.<br />As we Buddhists will tell you, it is very difficult to describe the indescribable but I will try. First of all life is humourous, the best part of life is laughter, and this book has plenty of that, and what is more uses it as a gentle didactic tool. Life is also a series of events, some of them make us despair, but there is always a solution, Tripitaka is almost an annoying characterin how he cries at every misfortune on the road, but that is to say we are also whiny and annoying and is to symbolise and recognise the suffering of humanity and the futility of worrying about it. The book is like many books of the west, written a century or so later, a picaresque work, but so much more entertaining, the adventures speed by. It is like a Buddhist "Pilgrims Progress" only with jokes and likability and a message which i prefer to that of Chaucer or Bunyan. <br />It makes a fair stab at Pure land Buddhism, and is inherently Chinese rather than Indian, you can see that with the vein of beauracracy and propriety running right through,Chinese Religions tend to encompass the others, so Confucianism is still there, (in fact I am surprised Li wasn't represented somewhere maybe it was and I missed it) and also with the specific brand of humour. <br />All the hidden as well as glaringly obvious religious messages aside, it is a wonderful story a glorious, ofetn jolly, romp, and I will be reading it to my kids with relish. <br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="8302323764327068242" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3019545273" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3019545273" />
    <a title="Cubex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/104334863-cubex"><img alt="Cubex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571622898p2/104334863.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3019545273?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cubex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cubex" href="/user/show/104334863-cubex">Cubex</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3019545273" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10653853862635861604">This book is a major classic of Chinese literature for good reason: it remains a gripping story with unforgettable characters. Arthur Waley is one of my favorite translators, and the fact that he was a skilled poet in his own right comes through in the simple beauty of the prose. I was a little disappointed to find that the translation skips a number of chapters from the original, but a friend of mine who's a fairly well-known translator of Chinese poetry told me that with a few exceptions, the </span>
  <span id="freeText10653853862635861604" style="display:none">This book is a major classic of Chinese literature for good reason: it remains a gripping story with unforgettable characters. Arthur Waley is one of my favorite translators, and the fact that he was a skilled poet in his own right comes through in the simple beauty of the prose. I was a little disappointed to find that the translation skips a number of chapters from the original, but a friend of mine who's a fairly well-known translator of Chinese poetry told me that with a few exceptions, the skipped chapters are too "culture-bound" to make much sense to a modern audience. In any case, the plot felt well-paced, and I found the unruly antics of the Monkey King and Pigsy so entertaining that's it was almost a shame to see Tripitaka nudging them toward enlightenment.<br /><br />I'm also a huge fan of two modern books that draw heavily on this story. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/118944.American_Born_Chinese" title="American Born Chinese by Gene Luen Yang" rel="nofollow noopener">American Born Chinese</a> is where I heard about this book in the first place, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/156683.The_Laughing_Sutra" title="The Laughing Sutra by Mark Salzman" rel="nofollow noopener">The Laughing Sutra</a> can almost be read as a sequel if this book leaves you wanting to read more adventures of the Monkey King.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10653853862635861604" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1552335043" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1552335043" />
    <a title="shanghao" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8606081-shanghao"><img alt="shanghao" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579963873p2/8606081.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1552335043?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="shanghao" class="user" itemprop="url" name="shanghao" href="/user/show/8606081-shanghao">shanghao</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780804842723" href="/book/show/12686951-the-monkey-king-s-amazing-adventures">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8606081-shanghao?shelf=orient">orient</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8606081-shanghao?shelf=ye-olde-days">ye-olde-days</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8606081-shanghao?shelf=fable">fable</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8606081-shanghao?shelf=translation">translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8606081-shanghao?shelf=spiritual">spiritual</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1552335043" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15124499233336117673">Readable introduction to one of the four Chinese literary epics. This one has an interesting preface and serves to give the reader a summarised version of events encountered by the group of protagonists.<br/><br/>What it doesn't do is capture the mystic charm of the original text (which admittedly is less accessible to English or casual Mandarin readers), or expound upon the profundity of the journey in its parts. A lot of the scenes ended almost as abruptly as they began and left me with questions hangin</span>
  <span id="freeText15124499233336117673" style="display:none">Readable introduction to one of the four Chinese literary epics. This one has an interesting preface and serves to give the reader a summarised version of events encountered by the group of protagonists.<br /><br />What it doesn't do is capture the mystic charm of the original text (which admittedly is less accessible to English or casual Mandarin readers), or expound upon the profundity of the journey in its parts. A lot of the scenes ended almost as abruptly as they began and left me with questions hanging by the tip of my tongue.<br /><br />It was 'Sun the Great Sage did this. Then the Master did that. Then such and such happened. Kthxbai.' almost on a loop. The poems were quite skillfully translated though , I must say.<br /><br />Well it's the Year of the Monkey after all, and reading this made me reminisce about the fun TV adaptations I've watched in my childhood. Also what initially piqued my interest was my involvement in translating a mobile game loosely based on the story. Maybe I'll check out more versions or muster up the grit to actually read the original (I merely skimmed the beginning chapters before resting; to say it's overwhelming in its meaning is an understatement)<br /><br />Happy Year of Monkey everyone!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15124499233336117673" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_416873639" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/416873639" />
    <a title="Karen Mosley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1178359-karen-mosley"><img alt="Karen Mosley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1340080777p2/1178359.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/416873639?book_show_action=true">Sep 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karen Mosley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karen Mosley" href="/user/show/1178359-karen-mosley">Karen Mosley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer416873639" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10681715479653841710">Because I was going to live and teach in China for a year, I wanted to be informed on classic Chinese literature. I started with "Dream of the Red Chamber" which was difficult to follow, with its 400+ characters, and numerous subplots. Then I began The Journey to the West about the famous Monkey King, Sun Wukong, who is a mischievous trouble-maker until he is trapped under a mountain for 500 years, converts to Buddhism, then begins a quest to protect Xuanzang (on his way to obtain scriptures fro</span>
  <span id="freeText10681715479653841710" style="display:none">Because I was going to live and teach in China for a year, I wanted to be informed on classic Chinese literature. I started with "Dream of the Red Chamber" which was difficult to follow, with its 400+ characters, and numerous subplots. Then I began The Journey to the West about the famous Monkey King, Sun Wukong, who is a mischievous trouble-maker until he is trapped under a mountain for 500 years, converts to Buddhism, then begins a quest to protect Xuanzang (on his way to obtain scriptures from India) as an atonement for his past sins. The Monkey King (an actual monkey)has magical powers, including a flying cloud and a magic cudgel, but he is a trouble-maker and is only controlled by a magic band around his head which can be tightened causing him unbearable headaches. In each chapter, the group meets up with new demons to defeat, and soon the plot became repetitive and I forced myself to read on. It is loosely based on some real events, and somewhat represents our own "journey to enlightenment". I'm glad I read it, however, as everywhere I went in China, I saw evidences of this Chinese superhero and his famous deeds. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10681715479653841710" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_31836781" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/31836781" />
    <a title="Ademption" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1045093-ademption"><img alt="Ademption" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1264349089p2/1045093.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/31836781?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ademption" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ademption" href="/user/show/1045093-ademption">Ademption</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">people who let their id run wild, superhero fetishists</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1045093-ademption?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1045093-ademption?shelf=chinese-literature">chinese-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer31836781" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15376717654541885985">This is an abbreviated version of the Chinese classic "Journey to the West." Imagine Neal Cassidy roaming around ancient China with actual powers. A dubious superhero who does whatever the fuck he wants. Monkey, the Trickster God, is assigned to guard a monk traveling to the west in search of fabled sutras. All of the action seems to follow this pattern: <br/><br/>1) The monk warns Monkey against something <br/>2) Greedy Monkey does whatever is prohibited <br/>3) the Monkey suffers and everyone must have an unexpec</span>
  <span id="freeText15376717654541885985" style="display:none">This is an abbreviated version of the Chinese classic "Journey to the West." Imagine Neal Cassidy roaming around ancient China with actual powers. A dubious superhero who does whatever the fuck he wants. Monkey, the Trickster God, is assigned to guard a monk traveling to the west in search of fabled sutras. All of the action seems to follow this pattern: <br /><br />1) The monk warns Monkey against something <br />2) Greedy Monkey does whatever is prohibited <br />3) the Monkey suffers and everyone must have an unexpected adventure <br />4) the Monkey saves the day, is chastised, and learns a lesson (very un-Cassidy-like)<br />5) Repeat 1-4 <br /><br />Despite the formula, the book is full of great hijinks, angry deities, and super powers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15376717654541885985" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer61405387" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14993757844295553250">This book is funny, witty, and allegorical. Somehow it survived a translation from Chinese to English, and the passing of 400 years (or something like 400 years, I'm not sure exactly when it was written). I'd highly recommend it to anyone who is interested in Chinese culture.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('X5c7O7UbakqDAxq3FylQNDFUajujSJxWwl8plQDyYuoSxUAW1NHpD07GVNC/gprB9e+EaJzexZ1gA5AhJDaJNQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('OVqGFfYIpO04DOJkLOKrBhhgTPMw2BWbTz1/5G9p8+N0CP04l8InqPXJrAOESWHz3NuioA9OTFDtYcZQS60YPA==')})
  }
